two  values  may  be  similar.  However,  for  low-rank  coals,  great 
differences  between  equilibrium  and  total  moisture  values  are 
common. 

Another  moisture  value  frequently  encountered  is  the  free  or 
surface  moisture.  It  represents  that  moisture  removable  by 
mechanical  means.  For  an  hvAb  coal,  the  surface  moisture  of 
+25-mm  (1-in.)  particles  is  only  1  or  2%.  For-1.2-mm  (-14- 
mesh**)  fines,  however,  25-30%  moisture  is  not  uncommon. 

Density:  Table  2  indicates  that  vitrinite  density  passes 
through  a  minimum.  At  70%  C,  vitrinite  density  is  -1.45 
g/cm3  (90.6  lb/ft3).  It  decreases  to  a  minimum  of  -1.25 
g/cm3  (78.1  Ib/ft3)  at  84-88%  C,  before  rising  sharply  to 
-1.80  g/cm3  (1 12.5  Ib/ft3)  at  97%  C.  For  vitrinites  of  the 
same  carbon  content,  the  densities  of  exinites  are  lower  and 
those  oif  micrinites  and,  especially,  fusinites  are  higher 
(Apian,  1988;  Van  Krevelen,  .1961). 

Forms  of  oxygen:  Considerable  controversy  exists  regarding 
the  forms  of  oxygen  in  coal  and  the  means  of  analyzing  for  them 
(Dryden,  1 963;  Van  Krevelen,  1 96 1 ).  The  values  in  Table  2  for 
carboxyl  (COOH)  and  hydroxyl  (OH,  both  phenol  and  alcohol) 
come  from  the  Penn  State  Coal  Data  Bank  and  a  variety  of  other 
sources  (Blom  et  al.,  1957;  Dryden,  1963;  Fuerstenau  et  al., 
1983;  Van  Krevelen,  1961;  Yarzebetal.,  1979).  Intheirpaper, 
Yarzeb  et  al.  gi  ve  the  OH  content  of  a  large  number  of  US  coals 
and  a  regression  equation  as  a  function  of  the  %C  in  the  coal. 

One  of  the  most  detailed  studies  of  oxygen  functional  groups 
in  coal  is  that  of  Blom  et  al.  (1957;  Van  Krevelen,  1961).  As 
shown  in  Table  3,  the  oxygen  functionalities  also  include  ether 
(C-O-C),  ketone  (C=O)  and  methoxy  (OCH3)  groups,  in  addi 
tion  to  OH  and  COOH.  These  data  are  in  general  agreement  with 
those  of  Table  2,  although  the  65%  C  coal  is  a  European  brown 
coal  not  listed  in  Table  2. 

As  the  total  oxygen  and  oxygen  functional  groups  increase, 
the  bituminous  coal  surface  becomes  progressively  more  polar. 
Its  hydrophobicity,  floatability  and  PZC  in  water  all  decrease. 

Coal  hydrophobicity:  Of  the  several  means  for  determining 
coal  hydrophobicity  (Apian  and  Arnold,  1991),  methods  based 
on  the  contact  angle  are  most  commonly  used  (Parekh  and 
Apian,  1978;  Gutierrez  et  al.,  1984).  These  include  the  captive 
bubble  (a  bubble  deposited  on  a  polished  surface  of  coal 
immersed  in  water  or  a  solution)  and  the  sessile  drop  (a  drop  of 
liquid  placed  on  the  polished  surface)  methods.  Figure  2  (based 
on  the  composite  data  of  Arnold  and  Apian,  1989;  Gutierrez  et 
al.,  1984;  Horsley  and  Smith,  1961;  Onlin  and  Apian,  1989) 
shows  that  hydrophobicity  is  correlated  with  %C. 

Note  that  the  captive  bubble  and  the  sessile  drop  methods 
give  different  values  for  the  contact  angle.  Gutierrez  et  al.  ( 1 984) 
believe  that  the  coal  surface  is  a  mosaic  of  hydrophilic  and 
strongly  and  weakly  hydrophobic  sites.  The  captive  bubble 
measures  only  the  strongly  hydrophobic  sites,  while  the  sessile 
drop  measures  the  total  (strong  plus  weak)  hydrophobic  sites. 

In  addition,  the  sessile  drop  method  shows  a  much  greater 
data  scatter,  although  the  measured  maximum  hydrophobicity

the  maceral  content  is  vitrinite  (or  huminite  in  low-rank  coal,

The  influence  of  the  source  and  genesis  factors  given  in  Table 
1  leads  to  some  variation  in  the  hydrophobicity  of  Western  and 
Eastern  coals  as  a  function  of  rank  parameters.  Also,  the  striking 
difference  in  the  hydrophobicity  of  Western  and  Eastern  resinites 
results  from  their  very  different  geneses. 

Zeta  potential  of  coal:  As  shown  in  Fig.  3,  the  effect  of 
changing  coal  rank  is  to  lower  the  point  of  zero  charge  (PZC) 
from  pH  .7-8  for  high-rank  (Ivb,  mvb)  coals  to  pH  ~2  for  low- 
rank  (sub,  lig)  coals,  with  the  intermediate  ranks  falling  in 
between  (Apian,  1988;  Qnlin,  1984;  Orilin  and  Apian,  1989). 
Wen  and  Sun  (1981)  have  demonstrated  that  continued  oxida 
tion  of  a  high-rank  coal  produces  a  similar  ,lo\vering  of  the  PZC. 
Thus,  oxidation  of  a  coal  makes  it  act  like  a  coal  of  lower  rank. 

Further  details  on  the  properties  of  coal  are  given  elsewhere 
(Elliott,  1981;  Lowry,  1963;  Van  Krevelen,  1961),  as  is  their 
relevance  to  coal  preparation  (Apian,  1988;  Leonard  and 
Hardinge,  1991). 

Properties  of  the  pyrite  and  ash-forming  minerals 

In  considering  a  flotation  process,  it  is  important  to  know  the 
properties  of  the  mineral  to  be  floated  (in  this  case,  coal)  and  the 
minerals  not  to  be  floated  (pyrite  and  the  ash-forming  minerals). 

Pyrite:  There  are  three  forms  of  sulfur  in  coal:  sulfatic, 
organic  and  pyritic.  The  sulfate  sulfur  is  usually  negligible 
(-0.02%  S),  except  where  gypsum  is  present  or  the  coal-source 
pyrite  has  been  oxidized.  The  organic  sulfur  is  inherent  to  the 
coal  structure  and  cannot  be  removed  by  physical  means.  This 
leaves  only  the  pyritic  (or  marcasitic)  sulfur  that  can  be  reduced 
by  preparation  means.  However,  if  the  pyrite  is  finely  dissemi 
nated,  locking  may  seriously  restrict  its  removal.  Research  at  the 
US  Bureau  of  Mines  (USBM)  has  demonstrated  that  the  pyritic 
sulfur removableby  physical  means  (gravity) isalinear function 
of  the  log  mean  size  of  the  pyrite  grains  (McCartney  et  al.,  1 969). 
Therefore,  a  crude  estimation  of  its  liberation  potential  can  be 
made  from  microscopic  data. 

Numerous  studies  (Chander  and  Apian,  1992;  Chemowsky

Fig.  4  —  Morphology  of  an  ore-source  pyrite  as  compared  to  five  coal-source  pyrites  (Esposito  et  al.,  1987). 


and  Lyon,  1972;  Esposito  et  al.,  1987;  Hirt  and  Apian,  1991) 
have  demonstrated  that  coal-source  pyrite  is  very  different  in  its 
properties  and  in  its  response  to  flotation  than  is  ore-source 
pyrite.  One  of  the  most  comprehensive  evaluations  of  the 
physical  properties  of  coal  pyrites,  as  compared  to  an  ore  pyrite, 
isthatofEspositoetal.  (1987),  given  in  Table  4.  From  this  table, 
it  can  be  noted  that  not  only  do  coal  pyrites  differ  from  ore  pyrite, 
they  differ  from  each  other. 

For  example,  anthracite  is  the  only  coal-source  pyrite  with  an 
apparent  specific  gravity  (sp  gr)  even  close  to  the  textbook  value 
of  5.01.  The  coal  pyrites  studied  were  all  p-type,  while  the  ore 
pyrite  was  n-type  (although  ore  pyrites  are  known  to  be  n-  and  p- 
type;Shuey,  1975).  The  Elaine  surface  area  (that  measures  only 
the  external  surface)  shows  essentially  no  variation.  However, 
the  BET  surface  area  (that  measures  the  internal  and  external 
surfaces)  shows  that  anthracite-source  pyrites  have  two  to  five 
times  the  surface  area  of  ore  pyrite,  while  bituminous  coal  pyrites 
are  eight  to  33  times  greater.  There  appears  to  be  no  consistent 
difference  in  the  optical  behavior  or  the  inherent  floatability  of 
these  pyrites.  Esposito  et  al.  ( 1 987)  also  noted  a  difference  in  the 
oxidation  rate  and  water  solubility  of  these  pyrites.  The  dis 
solved  iron  from  these  pyrites  correlated  reasonably  well  with 
pyrite  surface  areas  and  the  inverse  of  the  crystallite  size. 

One  of  the  biggest  differences  noted  from  one  coal-source 
pyrite  to  another  is  in  its  morphology.  Table  4  shows  five  distinct 
morphologies,  and  four  of  the  samples  demonstrate  four  or  more 
of  them.  The  variation  in  pyrite  morphology  may  be  seen  more 
dramatically  in  Fig.  4.  From  Fig.  4C-E,  it  is  obvious  why  these 
pyrites  have  such  high  surface  areas.  In  fact,  the  popcorn-like 
surface  of  the  Pittsburgh  seam  pyrite  (Fig.  4D)  results  in  an 
especially  large  surface  area.  A  reduced  bulk  pyrite  specific 
gravity  and  an  easy  dissolution  in  water  would  also  follow  from 
the  pyrite  morphology. 

Ash  mineralogy:  While  approximately  50  different  minerals 


are  found  associated  with  coal  (Mackowsky,  1975),  less  than  1 
of  these  minerals  are  found  in  substantial  quantities  and  wid 
association  with  coals.  Typically,  only  three  or  four  will  hav 
major  significance  for  a  given  coal.  The  coal  seam  is  found  t 
contain  mainly  kaolinite  clay,  with  lesser  amounts  of  illiti 
quartz  and  gypsum  (O'Gorman  and  Walker,  1972;  Aplai 
1988).  The  preparation  plant  blackwater  from  the  Easter 
United  States  contains  about  50%  illite  and  mixed-layer  illitc 
montmorillonite  clays,  with  lesser  amounts  of  kaolinite,  quan 
and  calcite  (Bradley  et  al.,  1980).  The  latter  mineralogy,  moi 
relevant  to  the  preparation  process,  differs  from  the  ash  mine: 
alogy  of  the  seam  because  of  the  overbreak  of  the  illite-ric 
Pennsylvanian  shales  during  mining.  Reflecting  their  differei 
genesis,  Western  coal  samples  were  found  to  contain  montm< 
rillonite  and  kaolinite  clays,  together  with  calcite,  dolomite  an 
feldspar  (Bradley  et  al.,  1980). 

It  is  important  to  note  the  prominence  of  several  of  the  cla 
minerals  in  nearly  all  raw  coal  samples.  Because  of  their  fir 
particle  size,  they  may  be  expected  to  act  like  the  fluid  durir 
flotation. 

Flotation  of  coal  and  its  associated  pyrite  and  ash-formir 
minerals 

Coal  flotation  —  The  problems  to  be  overcome 

Coal  flotation  is  a  coal  cleaning  process  involving  an  inte 
action  between  the  nature  of  coal  and  the  process  variations 
reagents,  operating  conditions,  machinery  and  process  typ 
Several  investigators  (Ragosti,  1 970;  Klimpel,  1 984)  have  calh 
attention  to  this  interaction,  and  it  has  been  a  hallmark  of  muc 
of  the  coal  flotation  work  done  by  the  author.  The  nature  of  tl 
coal  has  been  discussed  in  the  previous  section.  What  follov 
has  largely  been  restricted  to  the  froth  flotation  of  coal  in  tl 
mechanical,  subaeration  type  of  cell.  By  simple  extrapolatio

most  of  the  data  may  also  be  transferred  to  column  flotation,  and 
they  are  obviously  relevant  to  the  selective  agglomeration 
processes  as  well. 

Using  flotation  rate,  recovery,  concentrate  grade  and  selec- 
-tivity-as-the-principal^aluative"parameters7th^ftJllow  Ing  dis^~ 
cussion  will  address  the  problems  of  coal  flotation  and  the 
rejection  of  pyrite  and  the  ash-forming  minerals.  It  will  be 
heavily  weighted  toward  the  nature  of  the  coal  and  the  influence 
of  reagents  and  operating  conditions. 

Based  on  an  initial  compilation  by  Mitchell  (1948),  the  list  of 
potential  coal  flotation  problems  has  been  expanded  by  the 
author  (Apian,  1989)  to  include  the  following  15  items: 

•  indifferent  results  in  removing  sulfur  and  ash; 

•  difficulty  in  floating  low-rank  and  oxidized  coals; 

•  inability  to  make  clean  separations  with  the  finer  sizes; 

•  inability  to  clean  slurries  containing  a  high  percentage  of 
clay; 

•  problems  with  water  chemistry; 

•  competition  with  effective,  gravity,  coarse-cleaning  meth 
ods; 

•  low  economic  value  of  coal  historically; 

•  dewatering  problems; 

•  transportation  and  storage  problems  with  coal  fines; 

•  marketing  problems  with  coal  fines; 

•  inadequate  attention  to  the  process  in  the  plant; 

•  inefficient  use  of  capital  and  operating  monies; 

•  inadequate  attention  to  procedures,  machines  and  pro 
cesses; 

•  lack  of  an  adequate  R&D  program;  and 

•  inertia. 

Each  of  these  items  has  been  discussed  in  detail.  However, 
in  terms  of  those  that  can  be  addressed  by  the  experimentalist 
using  flotation,  the  most  important  ones  are  how  to  effectively 
float  all  ranks  of  coal  (including  oxidized  coals)  and  how  to 
achieve  the  selective  rejection  of  sulfur  and  ash. 

The  influence  of  coal  properties  on  coal  flotation 

Hydrophobicity:  Figure  2  is  a  good  guide  to  the  floatability 
of  coal.  If  the  captive  bubble  contact  angle  is  >~60°,  the  coal 
floats  readily  with  a  minimal  amount  of  frother.  The  other  coals 
with  a  finite  captive  bubble  contact  angle  are  readily  floated  with 
a  frother  and  a  small  amount  of  fuel  oil  collector.  This  oil 
addition  also  facilitates  the  flotation  of  reluctant-to-float  coarse, 
surface  oxidized,  less  hydrophobic  macerals  (like  fusinite)  or 
moderately  locked  particles.  The  sessile  drop  contact  angle  is  a 
better  predictor  of  the  flotation  of  coal  using  a  frother-collector 
system. 

Figure  5  indicates  that  the  amount  of  reagent  required  for  coal 
flotation  increases  logarithmically  as  the  %C  decreases  linearly 
(Xu  and  Apian,  1993).  Gutierrez  and  Apian  (1984)  have 
previously  noted  a  semi-logarithmic  relationship  between  the 
fuel  oil  collector  and  the  %C.  Brown  ( 1 962)  had  noted  a  similar 
correlation,  but  since  he  had  used  a  narrower  range  of  ranks  and 
a  creosote-type  frother-collector,  he  concluded  that  the  reagent 
use  increased  linearly  with  a  decreasing  %C. 

On  a  practical  basis,  coal  ranks  of  hv  Ab  and  above  use  <0.5 
kg/t  (1  Ib/st)  fuel  oil  collector  for  their  flotation.  For  hvBb  and 
hvCb  coals,  0.5-5  kg/t  (1-10  Ib/st)  fuel  oil  collector  are  used, 
while  sub  and  lig  coals  require  >5  kg/t  ( 1 0  Ib/st).  Some  of  these 
latter  coals  may  require  more  than  25  kg/t  (50  Ib/st)  oil.  Coal 
flotation  using  large  oil  additions  also  requires  special  proce 
dures  (emulsification,  high  solids  conditioning)  or  the  use  of  a

special  collector. 

As  might  be  anticipated  from  the  previous  discussion  o1 
maceral  floatability,  Western  resinite  is  highly  floatable 
(Klepetko,  1947;  Laros  and  Pick,  1981),  while  fusinite  is  much 
less  floatable  than  vitrinite  from  the  same  source  (Arnold  and 
Apian,  1989;  Olson  and  Apian,  1984).  Hirt  and  Apian  (1991; 
report  the  relative  floatability  of  various  coal  macerals  found  in 
an  hvAb  coal  from  Eastern  Kentucky  to  be:  pseudovitrinite 
(high  reflectance)  >  pseudovitrinite  (low  reflectance)  >  vitrinite 
(high  reflectance)  >  vitrinite  (low  reflectance)  =  micrinite  = 
exinite  =  semi-fusinite  >  Eastern  resinite  >  fusinite.  Note  the 
great  floatability  difference  between  Eastern  and  Western  resinites 
that  is  a  manifestation  of  their  genesis. 

In  general,  the  bottom  line  in  predicting  the  floatability  of  a 
coal  from  basic  hydrophobicity  data  is  the  hydrophobicity  of  the 
major  maceral,  vitrinite.  From  Fig.  2,  its  hydrophobicity,  and 
hence  its  floatability,  varies  in  a  regular  manner  with  ASTM 
rank. 

Oxidized  coal:  Oxidation  makes  a  coal  act  as  if  it  were  one 
of  lower  rank,  with  a  resulting  decrease  in  hydrophobicity  and 
floatability  (Gutierrez  and  Apian,  1984).  These  authors  used 
ambient  temperature  air  or  oxygen  sparging  of  the  ground  coal 
pulp  instead  of  the  furnace  oxidation  of  coal  used  by  mosl 
previous  investigators.  This  ambient  temperature  air  or  oxygen 
sparging  procedure  is  believed  to  be  more  relevant  to  the  actual 
situation  encountered  in  coal  stockpiling  or  preparation.  Highly 
hydrophobic  coals  (Ivb,  mvb  and  the  upper  range  of  hvAb; 
essentially  the  coking  grade  coals)  can  tolerate  some  oxidation 
without  serious  floatability  loss.  However,  lower  ranks  are 
strongly  affected  by  oxidation,  and  their  loss  of  floatability 
increases  progressively  with  a  decrease  in  rank. 

The  extent  of  oxidation  may  be  determined  by  using  a  boiling 
caustic  solution  to  extract  soluble  oxidized  products  from  the 
coal.  The  more  severe  the  oxidation,  the  darker  the  resulting 
filtrate.  This  test  was  developed  by  Lowenhaupt  and  Gray 
(1980)  for  coking  coals  and  has  been  extended  to  all  ranks  by 
Gutierrez  and  Apian  (1984). 

Humic  acids,  containing  a  plethora  of  COOH  and  OH 
groups,  are  dissolved  from  low-rank  and  surface-oxidized  coal 
particles.  Their  presence  can  seriously  depress  coal  flotation 
(Sun,  1954;  Arnold  and  Apian,  1986). 

Interestingly  enough,  anthracite  flotation  actually  im 
proves  with  gentle  oxidation  with  chemicals,  air  sparging  or

is  postulated  that  gentle  oxidation  produces  some  sites  that 
will  allow  for  hydrogen  or  other  bonding  with  frothing  and 
collecting  reagents. 

The  deleterious  effect  of  coal  oxidation  on  flotation  can 
often  be  cured  by  increasing  the  amount  of  fuel  oil  collector. 
Seriously  oxidized  coals  of  very  low  rank  will  require  large 
amounts  of  oil  or,  preferably,  the  use  of  a  specific  collector. 

Flotation  rate:  These  studies  have  been  strongly  emphasized 
in  our  research  program  since  its  inception  a  quarter  century  ago. 
Many  different  rate  equations  have  been  proposed  and  used 
(Apian,  1977;  Arnold  and  Apian,  1989a;  Dowling  et  al.,  1985; 
Rastogi,  1970),  and  more  than  one  dozen  of  them  fit  the  facts 
reasonably  well.  Based  on  experience  in  our  laboratories,  the 
preferred  equation  for  coal  flotation  is  the  classical,  first-order 
rate  equation: 


FU1-exp(-Kt)] 


(1) 


where: 


•  r  is  the  fractional  recovery  of  a  given  component  in  time 
t, 

•  Rx  is  its  ultimate  recovery,  and 

•  K  is  the  rate  constant. 

This  equation  seems  to  be  more  tolerant  of  imperfect  data 
and  the  vagaries  of  coal  flotation  than  do  the  other  models 
(Apian  and  Arnold,  1991).  Since  a  well  mineralized  and 
flowable  froth  is  not  always  produced  immediately  after  the 
onset  of  aeration,  there  may  be  a  lag  time  (Agar  et  al.,  1980; 
Arnold  and  Apian,  1989a;  Rastogi,  1 970;  Rastogi  and  Apian, 
1985).  This  lag-time  concept  may  be  compensated  for  by 
substituting  a  (t+0)  term  for  t  in  Eq.  (1). 

It  should  be  cautioned  that  because  the  various  rate  equations 
are  formulated  differendy,  the  values  of  K  may  only  be  com 
pared  to  those  derived  from  the  same  equation. 

Differences  in  the  flotation  rates  of  the  various  species 
offer.an  excellent  opportunity  to  achieve  selectivity  between 
dissimilar  species  (Apian,  1977;  Rastogi  and  Apian,  1985). 
From  the  basic  time-recovery  data  of  Fig.  6  (Purcell  and 
Apian,  1991),  the  calculated  rate  constants  for  yield  and 
BTU  recovery  are  -1.75  min-',  total  sulfur  (ST)  is  0.98 
mhr1,  liberated  pyritic  sulfur  (SLP)  is  0.56  min~'  and  ash  is 
0.98  min"1.  Although  their  flotation  rates  are  similar,  the 
BTU  recovery  curve  is  greater  than  the  yield  curve  because 


of  the  presence  of  -15%  easily  rejectable  ash.  The  flotation 
rate  based  on 'yield  is  usually  a  good  proxy  for  that  of  the  coal 
(BTU)  itself. 

Particle  size:  As  might  be  expected,  various  coal  particles 
float  differently.  The  situation  is  greatly  complicated  by  the  coal 
rank,  the  particle  size  range  treated,  the  operating  variables  and 
the  reagent  system.  Crawford  (1936)  demonstrated  that  fine 
(-74  (im,  -200  mesh)  coal  particles  float  first,  followed  by  the 
intermediate  (295  um  x  74  urn,  48  x  200mesh)  sizes  and,  finally, 
the  coarse  (+295  (im,  +48  mesh)  particles.  Brown  and  Smith 
(1954-55)  and  Rastogi  and  Apian  (1985)  have  quantified  this 
sequence  using  rate  data. 

More  recently,  Raleigh  and  Apian  (1991)  have  shown  thai 
the  top  size  treated  plays  an  important  role  in  the  relative 
floatability  of  coal  particles.  For  several  coals,  they  found  thai 
as  the  top  size  was  reduced  from  -589  (im  (-28  mesh)  to  -147 
urn  (-100  mesh),  floatability  increased.  However,  when  it  wa5 
reduced  further  to  -37  |im  (—400  mesh),  the  floatability  de 
creased.  This  latter  decrease  was  attributed  to  the  difficulties  o1 
many  fine  particles  competing  for  the  bubble  surface  and  to  ar 
increased  probability  of  coal  surface  oxidation. 

Several  studies  (Olson  and  Apian,  1984, 1987;  Rastogi  anc 
Apian,  1 985)  have  demonstrated  that  when  an  excessive  amoun 
of  oil  is  used,  all  of  the  particles  tend  to  float  at  about  the  sairu 
rate. 

To  facilitate  the  flotation  of  the  coarser  coal  particles,  mon 
oily  collector  is  often  used.  While  this  improves  the  yield,  it  alsc 
increases  the  floatability  of  the  undesirable,  fine  particles  o 
pyrite  and  ash  (Apian,  1977;  Olson  and  Apian,  1984, 1987;  Hir 
and  Apian,  1991). 

Correlation  with  specific  gravity  separations:  Specifn 
gravity  separations,  available  from  the  standard  coal  washabil 
ity  data,  provide  a  quick  approximation  as  to  how  the  variou 
raw  coal  particles  might  respond  in  a  flotation  system.  Liki 
gravity  processes,  as  the  quantity  of  ±0. 1  sp  gr  material  (lockei 
particles  and  fusinite)  increases  above  10%,  flotation  separa 
tions  may  also  be  expected  to  be  difficult.  Of  course,  flotatioi 
is  even  more  complex  than  gravity  separations,  because  fiota 
tion  must  also  confront  the  complications  of  poorly  fioatabl< 
particles,  floatable  gangue  minerals  and  those  accidental!; 
collected  by  the  reagent  system  used. 

Besides  the  use  of  washability  data  as  a  guide,  a  grade-yieli 
curve,  developed  from  several  flotation  tests  (Fig.  7),  will  givi 
the  experimentalist  or  plant  operator  a  good  idea  of  the  trade-of 
between  coal  recovery  and  the  contamination  of  the  clean  COB 


90    JANUARY  1993 


MINING  ENGINEERS 


212 

product  by  sulfur  and  ash.  An  easy  separation  is  represented  in 
Fig.  7  A,  while  Fig.  7B  shows  a  difficult  one. 

Influence  of  reagents 

The  cardinal  rule  here  is  that  an  excess  of  any  reagent  is 
invariably  deleterious  to  the  flotation  process  in  terms  of  the 
selectivity  between  the  coal  and  the  gangue  minerals.  In 
addition  to  achieving  a  high  coal  recovery,  an  excess  of  frother 
or  collector  will  usually  encourage  a  gangue  mineral  flotation. 
Furthermore,  an  excess  of  a  gangue  depressant  will  likely 
depress  the  coal. 

Frothers:  The  most  commonly  used  coal  frothers  have  been 
the  short,  branched-chain  alcohols,  and  the  creosotes,  pine  oils 
and  polypropylene  glycol  ethers.  Their  principal  function  is  to 
create  bubbles,  but  some  of  them  also  collect  coal  orpromote  the 
flotation  of  pyrite  and  ash.  MIBC  (methyl  isobutyl  carbinol,  4- 
methyl-2-pentanol)  is  usually  considered  to  be  a  benign  frother 
in  the  flotation  of  the  higher-rank  coals,  but  an  excess  has  been 
demonstrated  to  greatly  encourage  the  flotation  of  pyrite  and  ash 
(Apian,  1976;  Bonner  and  Apian,  1992;  Olson  and  Apian, 
1 987).  Creosotes  and  pine  oils  are  essentially  obsolete,  but  they 
have  collecting  properties  for  coal  and  often  for  the  gangue 
minerals.  The  newer  glycol  frothers  offer  advantages  in  floating 
coarse  coal  particles  (Klimpel,  1992).  Unfortunately,  several  of 
these  may  strongly  adsorb  onto  the  coal,  requiring  a  greater 
quantity  to  achieve  good  frothing.  The  prior  addition  of  oil, 
however,  mitigates  this  problem  by  blocking  the  coal  surface  to 
frother  adsorption. 

Collectors:  Their  principal  function  is  to  improve  the 
hydrophobicity  and,  hence,  the  floatability  of  coal  particles. 
Preferably,  they  should  not  also  encourage  gangue  mineral 
flotation.  The  most  commonly  used  collector  for  coal  flotation 
is  No.  2  fuel  oil,  for  reasons  of  effectiveness,  cost,  availability 
and  convenience.  Kerosene  is  used  to  a  lesser  extent,  and  other 
collectors  are  used  only  sparingly.  Collector  use  is  mandatory 
for  the  flotation  of  low-rank  and  oxidized  bituminous  coals. 

The  study  of  oil's  influence  on  coal  flotation  has  represented 
a  major  thrust  in  our  research  (Apian,  1 983;  Bonner  and  Apian, 
1992;  Gutierrez  and  Apian,  1984;  Olson  and  Apian,  1984, 1987; 
Onlin  and  Apian,  1989;  Xu  and  Apian,  1993).  Based  on  these 
studies,  the  advantages  and  disadvantages  of  No.  2  fuel  oil  are 
given  in  Table  5.  The  inescapable  conclusion  is  that  while  this 
reagent  is  an  excellent  coal  collector,  it  also  encourages  the 
flotation  of  pyrite  and  ash-forming  minerals.  In  addition,  unless 
used  judiciously,  it  can  lead  to  a  serious  selectivity  loss.

authors  concl  uded  that  for  coal  flotation ,  per  se,  No.  2  fuel  oil  i

this  generalization  is  the  flotation  of  very  low-rank  and  highl;

One  of  the  most  detailed  studies  of  coal  collection  reporta 
in  the  literature  is  that  of  Mainhopd  and  Whelan  ( 1 955).  Mor< 
recently  Onlin  and  Apian  (1989;  1993)  have  made  a  detalio 
study  of  the  collection  of  coals,  ranging  from  anthracite  ti 
lignite,  and  of  oxidized  coals,  Some  of  the  better  collectors  fo 
the  low-rank  and  oxidized  coals,  in  addition  to  fuel  oil,  includ' 
dodecane,  tricresyl  phosphate,  tributyl  phosphite  and  som* 
phenols,  carboxy  lie  acids  and  amines  (Apian  and  Arnold,  1 99 1 ) 

In  summary,  theoretically,  no  collector  is  required  for  high 
rank  (>~86%  C)  bituminous  coals,  although,  in  reality,  a  sma! 
amount  of  oil  is  commonly  used  in  their  flotation.  No  singl 
collector  is  best  for  all  ranks  of  coal,  but  No.  2  fuel  oil  comes  th< 
closest  to  being  universally  effective.  For  low-rank  and  oxi 
dized  coals,  the  use  of  one  qf  the  more  exotic  collectors  give: 
above  may  be  required. 

pH:  The  classic  work  on  the  effect  of  pH  on  coal  flotation  i 
that  of  Zimmerman  (1948).  Working  with  an  hvAb  coal,  h< 
found  a  roughly  parabolic,  pH -recovery  relationship.  The  opti 
mum  recovery  was  near  pH  7-8.  It  dropped  off  sharply  belov 
pH  ~4  and  above  pH  ~9. 

More  recent  work  in  our  laboratories  (Xu  and  Apian,  1993 
finds  that  this  representation  is  only  approximately  true.  High 
rank  Ivb  and  mvb  coals  float  well  over  a  broad  pH  range  fron 
pH  -3-11,  centered  around  pH  7-8  as  an  optimum.  Th' 
Zimmerman  plot  is  a  good  representation  of  an  hv  Ab  coal.  A 
the  coal  rank  is  progressively  reduced,  the  amount  of  oil  requirei 
forflotation  increases,  while  the  region  of  maximum  floatabilit; 
is  progressively  reduced  to  pH  -4.  Simultaneously,  coal  recov 
ery  and  the  pH  range  of  floatability  also  decrease.  A  sub-( 
bituminous  coal  was  found  to  require  either  a  massive  amoun 
of  oil  or  the  use  of  a  special  collector  (naphthenic  acid),  as  seei 
in  Fig.  5. 

Coal  depression:  In  some  instances,  it  may  be  desirable  t< 
float  the  pyrite  and/or  ash  minerals  and  depress  the  coal.  A  largi 
number  of  depressant  systems  have  been  evaluated  in  ou 
laboratory,  and  Im  and  Apian  (1981)  have  concluded  that ; 
dextrine  (com,  wheat,  potato,  tapioca,  etc.)  is  the  most  cos 
effective  coal  depressant.  An  extensive  study  of  the  adsorptioi 
and  function  of  the  various  polysaccharides  has  been  made  b; 
Perry  and  Apian  (1988)  and  by  Esposito-Brick  and  Aplai 
(1992). 

Influence  of  other  process  variables 

Several  of  the  important  process  variables,  such  as  frothe 
and  collector  concentration,  %  solids,  aeration,  agitation  ant 
conditioning,  have  been  studied  in  our  laboratories.  The  pro 
gressive  increase  in  the  frother  and/or  collector  will  improv< 
coal  recovery  at  the  expense  of  a  substantial  increase  in  th< 
flotation  of  pyrite  and  ash  (Bonner  and  Apian,  1992;  Hirt  am 
Apian,  1991;  Olson  and  Apian,  1984, 1987;  Rastogi  and  Apian 
1985;  Xu  and  Apian,  1993).  Excess  reagent  encourages  th< 
flotation  of  the  gangue  constituents  more  than  it  does  thi 
flotation  of  coal.  A  starvation  or  slightly  higher  quantity  of ; 
given  reagent  usually  represents  the  best  trade-off.

Aeration  has  been  found  to  be  a  significant  variable  in 
flotation  (Hirt  and  Apian,  1 99  l;Rastogi  and  Apian,  1985).  Also, 
increased  airflow  is  found  to  increase  the  coal  flotation  rate  and 
recovery,  as  well  as  the  flotation  of  the  coarser  (~589  urn,  -28 
fnesh)  coal  pardcleTrTn^ttotariprrrateris-rdated-to-the-airflovr 
rate,  Q,  by  the  approximation: 

K  a  Q°-79  (2) 

Pulp  agitation  is  strongly  influenced  by  the  impeller  speed, 
and  aeration  and  agitation  are  related  by  the  dimensionless 
airflow  number,  NQ, 


NQ  =  Q/ND3 


(3) 


where: 


•  N  is  the  impeller  speed,  and 

•  D  is  the  impeller  diameter. 

Rastogi  and  Apian  ( 1 985)  have  shown  an  optimum  in  coal  yield 
at  NQ  ~4  x  10~2,  very  similar  to  that  noted  for  ore  flotation 
(Arbiter  et  al.,  1968).  Unfortunately  this  optimum  in  NQ  also 
corresponds  to  the  poorest  rejection  of  pyritic  sulfur  (Hirt  and 
Apian,  1991). 

For  a  variety  of  operational  reasons,  coal  flotation  is  usually 
done  industrially  at  3-5%  solids,  although  Crawford  (1936)  has 
found  the  optimum  for  coal  flotation  to  be  at  12%  solids. 
Thickening  of  the  dilute  preparation-plant  pulp  before  flotation 
is  exceedingly  rare  and  is  usually  found  only  in  those  plants 
designed  by  ore  dressing  engineers.  Evaluating  the  range  of  3.6- 
1 7.2%  solids,  Rastogi  and  Apian  ( 1 985)  found  that  the  coal  yield 
increased  with  an  increase  in  the  solids.  This  could  probably  be 
attributed  to  a  better  recovery  of  the  coarser  particles.  However,  the 
coal  flotation  rate  and  the  pyrite  rejection  were  best  at  ~9%  solids. 

Conditioning  of  the  coal  slurry  with  reagents  is  rarely  prac 
ticed  as  a  separate  unit  operation,  and  the  reagents  are  often 
added  to  a  pump  sump  or  a  feed  box  ahead  of  the  flotation 
process.  For  the  flotation  of  low-rank  and  oxidized  coals,  or  to 
promote  the  flotation  of  relatively  coarse  particles  (>~589  |Jjn, 
>~28  mesh),  a  separate  conditioning  step  is  helpful.  For  very 
low-rank  coals,  it  is  a  virtual  necessity,  especially  to  disperse  and 
distribute  the  large  amount  of  oil  used  as  a  collector.  High-solids 
conditioning  and  pre-emulsification  of  the  oil  in  water,  or  in  a 
water-surfactant  solution,  can  substantially  improve  the  coal 
recovery  and  reduce  the  amount  of  oily  collector  needed  to  float 
lower-rank  coals,  such  as  hvcb  and  sub  (Apian,  1983;  Xu  and 
Apian,  1993). 

The  best  rejection  of  pyrite  (and  by  implication,  ash)  is 
achieved  under  the  gentle  operating  conditions  of  low  air,  low 
rpm  and  low  reagent  levels. 

The  major  defect  in  most  of  these  studies  of  process  variables 
is  that  one  variable  at  a  time  is  evaluated  while  the  others  are  held 
constant.  Our  current  research  (Ranjan  and  Apian,  1993)  is  to 
look  at  interactions  due  to  changes  in  the  variables  of  frother  and 
collector  concentration,  airflow  rate,  grind  size  and  solids. 

Selectivity  against  pyrite  and  the  ash-forming  minerals 

The  rejection  of  gangue  minerals  can  be  achieved  by  control, 
of  reagents  (frothers,  collectors  and  gangue  depressants),  oper 
ating  variables  and  the  flotation  rate  of  the  various  species.  In 
general,  the  reduction  of  the  gangue  minerals  during  coal 
flotation  may  be  achieved  by: 

•    depressing  the  pyrite  (or  ash  minerals)  and  floating  the 
coal; 


•  depressing  thecoal  and  floating  the  pyriteorash  minerals; 

•  improved  flotation  circuitry  (cleaners,  scavengers,  split- 
feed  flotation,  etc.); 

•  froth  sprinkling; 

• — improved-maehines-andproeesses-(-modified  mechanical- 

cells,  column  flotation,  bubble  size  control,  flocculation, 
etc.);  and 

•  alternative  machines  and  processes  (bubble  techniques, 
agglomeration  and  selective  flocculation  techniques). 

Research  in  our  laboratories  has  generally  focused  on  the  use 
of  conventional,  mechanical  flotation  cells  and  circuits.  The 
goal  has  been  to  develop  an  understanding  of  how  the  various 
components  respond  to  process  changes  and  how  selectivity 
between  coal  and  gangue  may  be  achieved  through  the  use  of 
specific  depressants  for  the  pyrite  or  ash  minerals.  It  is  the 
author's  belief,  however,  that  all  reasonable  avenues  should  be 
explored,  such  as  the  use  of  froth  sprinkling  and  column  flotation 
that  are  especially  appealing  for  cleaning  use.  These  offer  very 
attractive  research  targets,  as  do  the  flotation  of  gangue  minerals 
away  from  the  coal  and  the  use  of  agglomerative  techniques. 

Pyrite  rejection:  The  unintentional  flotation  of  pyrite  during 
coal  flotation  is  caused  by  locked  particles,  fine  pyrite  particles, 
prolonged  flotation  time,  excessive  frother  and/or  collector  and 
conditions  promoting  the  natural  floatability  of  pyrite.  In  this 
latter  case,  the  formation  of  elemental  sulfur  on  the  pyrite 
surface  is  controlled  by  the  Eh  of  the  system  (Chander  et  al., 
1992).  In  general,  pyrite  is  best  rejected  during  coal  flotation  by 
an  "easy  does  it"  approach,  but  a  more  exhaustive  listing  would 
include  these  comments  and  conditions: 

•  coarse  pyrite  (>295  ujn,  48  mesh)  particles  do  not  floai 
well  under  typical  coal  flotation  conditions 

•  good  liberation,  few  locked  particles 

•  short  flotation  times 

•  gentle  flotation  conditions: 

•  starvation  frother  and  collector 

•  nonoily  reagents 

•  low  aeration 

•  low  impeller  speeds 

•  appropriate  circuitry  (split  feed,  two-stage  reverse,  "gral 
and  run,"  froth  sprinkling,  etc.) 

•  appropriate  column  flotation 

Pyrite  rejection  is  especially  difficult  because  of  a  variatioi 
in  its  floatability  from  source  to  source.  Hirt  and  Apian  (1991 
found  that  several  coal-source  pyrites  floated  faster  than  an  ore 
source  pyrite  (2.3  to  5.7  times  faster).  Also,  there  was  a  wid> 
variation  in  the  floatability  of  coal  pyrite,  depending  on  it 
source. 

In  addition  to  the  above  procedures,  it  is  possible  to  add 
specific  pyrite  depressant.  Historically,  only  a  few  studies  c 
pyrite  depression  during  coal  flotation  have  been  made  (Millei 
1964;  Yancey  and  Taylor,  1935).  Studies  in  our  laboratorie 
include:  Arnold  and  Apian,  1990a;  Chander  and  Apian,  199; 
Choudhry  and  Apian,  1992;  Esposito-Brick  and  Apian,  199/ 
Perry  and  Apian,  1985, 1988;  and  Raleigh  and  Apian,  1991. 

Choudhry  and  Apian  (1992)  have  listed  the  following  re 
agent  depression  systems: 

•  alkalis, 

•  lime  and  Ca"1"2  compounds,       •* 

•  oxidizing  reagents," 

•  reducing  reagents, 

•  specific  inorganic  ions, 


92    JANUARY  1993 


MINING  ENGINEERS 


214 

•  dispersing  agents, 

•  natural  and  synthetic  polymers, 

•  xanthated  polymers  and  similar  compounds, 

•  special  thiol  compounds, 

•  pyrite  surface  alteration  followed~by  the  use  of  a  specific" 
depressant  for  this  new  surface,  and 

•  dyes  and  chelation  compounds. 

In  considering  pyrite  depression,  the  experimentalist  should 
know  that:  . 

•  While  coal  rank  dictates  the  frother-collector  system, 
these  reagents  may  also  promote  pyrite  flotation  (oily  re 
agents  are  especially  bad). 

•  The  author  and  his  associates  have  found  no  one  best 
pyrite  depressant.  The  best  pyrite  depressant  for  one  coal  is 
not  necessarily  the  best  for  another  coal. 

•  All  pyrite  depressants  tested  in  our  laboratories  to  date 
also  depress  coal  at  a  similar  or  (usually)  greater  concentra 
tion. 

•  Some  pyrite  depressants,  especially  the  natural  and 
synthetic  polymers,  may  also  improve  coal  floatability 
slightly. 

Rejection  of  ash-forming  minerals:  Clays  and  other  fine 
particles  enter  the  clean  coal  froth  principally  through  the 
mechanism  of  froth  carry-over.  Arnold  and  Apian  (1986) 
demonstrated  that  the  amount  of  kaolinite  or  illite  clay 
entering  the  froth  is  roughly  what  one  would  expect  from  the 
amount  of  water  reporting  to  the  froth  and  assuming  that 
complete  mixing  occurs  in  the  flotation  cell.  When  the  clay 
is  montmorillonite  (bentonite),  however,  much  more  enters 
the  froth,  and  this  clay  can  seriously  depress  coal  flotation. 
As  a  consequence,  the  flotation  of  Western  coals,  which 
commonly  contain  montmorillonite  clay,  may  pose  a  serious 
challenge  to  the  preparation  engineer. 

In  addition  to  froth  carry-over,  ash  particles  may  enter  the 
froth  through  the  agglomeration  of  dissimilar  species,  "piggy 
back"  attachment  to  coal  particles,  accidental  entrapment  in 
the  froth  mass,  specific  collection  and  the  hierarchy  of  their 
flotation  rates  as  a  function  of  particle  size.  Fine  pyrite,  for 
example,  tends  to  float  at  about  the  same  rate  as  coarse  coal 
particles  (Arnold  and  Apian,  1 990a;  Olson  and  Apian,  1 984; 
Purcell  and  Apian  1991). 

The  deleterious  effect  of  oil  may  be  seen  from  the  work  of 
Olson  and  Apian  (1987).  Using  an  Ivb  coal,  they  found  that 
even  after  eight  minutes  of  flotation  using  a  starvation 
quality  of  MIBC  (0. 10  kg/t,  0.2  Ib/st),  -35%  of  the  pyrite  and 
53%  of  the  ash  were  floated.  In  the  first  minute  of  flotation, 
most  of  the  pyrite  and  ash  particles  floated  were  those 
particles  <37  |im  (400  mesh).  By  contrast,  when  0.35  kg/t 
(0.7  Ib/st)  of  oil  was  added  (greatly  excessive),  the  yield 
increased  at  the  expense  of  a  substantial  flotation  of  the 
pyrite  and  ash  constituents.  Most  of  these  floated  in  one 
minute,  including  pyrite  particles  as  coarse  as  589  x  208  \im 
(28x65  mesh)  and  ash  particles  as  coarse  as  1 . 1 7  x  0.5  89  mm 
(14x28  mesh). 

Circuitry:  A  large  number  of  circuits  have  been  exam 
ined,  including  various  combinations  of  roughing,  cleaning, 
scavenging,  two-stage  reverse,  "grab  and  run"  and  split-feed 
flotation  (Apian,  1976;  Apian  and  Arnold,  1991;  Bensley 
and  Nicol,  1985;  Bonner  and  Apian,  1992;  Purcell  and 
Apian,  1993).  The  most  effective  circuits  for  coal  recovery 
and  the  rejection  of  gangue  appear  to  be  the  "grab  and  run," 
two-stage  reverse,  split  feed  or  simple  cleaning  varieties.

fusinite  (high  ash)  or  pyrite  may  be  improved  by  using  minimal

particles  >2Q8  |im  (65  mesh),  the  1 .4  float  fraction  is  generally 
devoid  of  high-ash  fusinite  and  particles  containing  visible  free 
ash.  Not  many  of  the  >208  Lim  (65  mesh)  particles  in  the  1.4  x 
1 .7  sp  gr  fraction  float  under  these  same  conditions.  Reference 
to  Table  6  indicates  that  flotation  can  be  used  to  minimize  the 
flotation  of  the  coarser  locked  particles. 

Liberation:  For  those  wishing  to  evaluate  the  effect  of 
comminution  on  pyrite  and  ash  liberation,  the  work  of  McCartney 
et  al.  ( 1 969)  may  be  used  as  an  estimate.  In  addition,  Perez  and 
Apian  (1987)  have  developed  a  simple  model  to  represent  the 
fractional  yield  of  a  species  (like  pyrite  or  ash)  out  of  a  coal-ash- 
pyrite  assemblage. 

The  fractional  yield  of  species,  A,  is: 


D.C.  Click,  Energy  and  Fuels  Research  Center,  Penn  Stai 
University,  who  supplied,  from  the  Penn  State  Coal  Data  Banl 
much  of  the  data  used  to  develop  Table  2. 


A  =  YA(X)  *  JIT** 


(4) 


where: 


•  x  is  the  particle  size,  and 

•  a  and  \i  are  constants. 

Summary 

Coal  flotation  is  a  complicated  process,  but  it  is  subject  to 
rational  interpretation.  Very  fortunately,  coal  properties  are 
usually  dependent  upon  rank  parameters,  as  is  coal  flotation. 
The  following  guidelines  should  be  helpful: 

•  Know  your  coal. 

•  Know  the  general  geologic  and  geographic  genesis  and 
location  information  and  the  special  properties  associated 
with  these  circumstances. 

•  Know  the  coal  rank.  From  this,  its  physical  and  chemical 
properties  may  be  inferred. 

•  Know  the  sulfur  content  and  forms — pyritic,  organic  and 
sulfate. 

•  Consider  any  special  circumstances  such  as: 

•  oxidation, 

•  pyrite  and  ash  mineral  constituents,  their  quantity, 
distribution  and  properties,  and 

•  user  constraints. 

•  Know  the  strengths  and  weaknesses  of  the  flotation 
processes  and  the  reagents  used. 

•  Know  the  user  constraints  so  that  grade-yield  trade-offs 
can  be  properly  evaluated. »

Eleanor  Herz  Swent 


Born  in  Lead,  South  Dakota,  where  her  father  became  chief  metallurgist  for 
the  Homestake  Mining  Company.  Her  mother  was  a  high  school  geology 
teacher  before  marriage. 

Attended  schools  in  Lead,  South  Dakota,  Dana  Hall  School,  and  Wellesley 
College,  Massachusetts.  Phi  Beta  Kappa.  M.A.  in  English,  University  of 
Denver.  Assistant  to  the  President,  Elmira  College,  New  York.  Married  to 
Langan  Waterman  Swent,  mining  engineer. 

Since  marriage  has  lived  in  Tayoltita,  Durango,  Mexico;  Lead,  South  Dakota; 
Grants,  New  Mexico;  Piedmont,  California. 

Teacher  of  English  as  a  Second  Language  to  adults  in  the  Oakland,  California 
public  schools.  Author  of  an  independent  oral  history  project,  Newcomers  to 
the  East  Bay,  interviews  with  Asian  refugees  and  immigrants.  Oral  historian 
for  the  Oakland  Neighborhood  History  Project. 

Interviewer,  Regional  Oral  History  Office  since  1985,  specializing  in  mining 
history.  In  1998,  awarded  an  honorary  Doctor  of  Laws  degree  by  South 
Dakota  School  of  Mines  and  Technology.
